1001 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. De Long


Crim. No. 8274


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


11 Cal. App. 3d 786; 90 Cal. Rptr. 193; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1779



September 30, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 25, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, Martin E. Rothenberg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a conviction by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) for possession of marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, where the marijuana was in plain view after police officers opened the trunk of appellant's automobile to determine whether appellant violated gun control statutes codified at Cal. Penal Code §§ 171e, 12031.


OVERVIEW: A police officer observed appellant put two firearms in the trunk of appellant's car on a junior college campus. Acting under the authority of Cal. Penal Code §§171c, 12031, the police officer opened the trunk to determine if the guns were loaded because the guns were prohibited in that area. While examining the guns, the officer discovered marijuana in plain view in the trunk. Appellant was convicted only for possession of the marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530 and appellant sought review on the claim that the initial search for the guns was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the conviction and held that the mere examination of the guns to determine if they were loaded was not an unreasonable search because the presence of the guns was prohibited by the college. Because the officers were not investigating a crime and were only complying with §§171c, 12031, the intrusion on appellants was minimal, there was a potential threat to public safety at the junior college due to the guns, and the statutes were applied equally, the court held that the examination for the guns was reasonable, and therefore, the seizure of the marijuana was also proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for possession of marijuana and held that where there was an examination of the guns to determine if they were loaded and where the guns were prohibited in the junior college area, the examination of the guns was not unreasonable. Therefore, the seizure of the marijuana found in plain view during the search was also proper.

 

1002 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Candlestick Properties, Inc. v. San Francisco Bay Conservation Etc. Com.


Civ. No. 26216


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


11 Cal. App. 3d 557; 89 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1755; 3 ELR 20446



September 24, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 23, 1970, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1970. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landowner sought review of an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5 to review a decision of respondent development commission that denied appellant's application for a permit to fill a parcel of land.


OVERVIEW: Appellant owned land within the boundaries of a state reclamation district that it contended was worthless except as a place to deposit construction fill. Appellant applied to respondent development commission for a permit to fill the property, which was denied. Appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate and damages for an alleged taking of its property without just compensation. The petition for the writ of mandate was denied and a demurrer without leave to amend was sustained. Appellant sought review and the court affirmed, holding that respondent had proper authority to deny appellant's application as part of the essential public interest and compelling economic necessity supporting the creation of the reclamation district, as enunciated in Cal. Gov't Code §§ 66600, 66603, and 66604. The court found that the provisions of Cal. Gov't Code § 66622, which allowed commission members to designate proxies to act and vote in their place, was a constitutional delegation of legislative power. The court also found that the legislation that created respondent was a valid exercise of police power, and thus its actions did not constitute a taking of private property.


OUTCOME: The decision to deny appellant's petition for a writ of mandate and damages for a taking of private property without just compensation was affirmed. The court held that respondent development commission exercised proper authority in denying appellant's request to fill land, and that its actions in regulating the land fill were not a "taking" of private property without just compensation.

 

1003 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Strutt v. Ontario Sav. & Loan Assn.


Civ. No. 9477


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


11 Cal. App. 3d 547; 90 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1754



September 23, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 9, 1970, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, William L. Murray, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former owner appealed the summary judgment granted by the Superior Court of Orange County (California) to defendant current owner, in plaintiff's quiet title action against defendants, current owner, bank, and title insurer. The action alleged that defendants, bank and insurer, committed fraud in declaring plaintiff's promissory note in default, and that the foreclosure was invalid because of plaintiff's legal incompetence.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff former owner purchased an apartment building, assuming a promissory note in favor of defendant bank and a deed of trust in favor of defendant title company. After plaintiff defaulted on the note, defendant title company sold the realty under its deed of trust to defendant bank, which in turn sold it to defendant current owner. Plaintiff's action alleged that he was declared legally insane under Cal. Penal Code § 1368 prior to his default on the note, that defendants, bank and title company, had notice of this before it declared default and foreclosed on the realty, thereby invalidating the trustee's sale. Given that, plaintiff alleged that the sale to defendant current owner was also invalid. In affirming the judgment, the court found that it did not need to resolve any of the issues regarding defendants, bank and title company. The court found that the evidence showed that defendant current owner had no notice of plaintiff's alleged disability, was not chargeable with notice of such, and was in no way involved in either the default or foreclosure sale, but was a bona fide purchaser for value whose title was not subject to attack.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment because defendant current owner of property was a bona fide purchaser for value with no notice of any fraud or other illegality that either defendant bank or defendant title insurer may have perpetrated upon plaintiff former owner. Defendant was not chargeable with notice of plaintiff's alleged disability and there was no due process violation in the notice provision of the foreclosure statute.

 

1004 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Slaughter v. Edwards


Civ. No. 26370


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


11 Cal. App. 3d 285; 90 Cal. Rptr. 144; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1732



September 18, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Solano County, Ellis R. Randall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The automatic suspension of defendant's license pursuant to section 10475 is annulled. The order appealed from is affirmed. Each party shall bear his own costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant real estate broker sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California), which granted respondent judgment creditor's application for an order directing payment of a prior judgment for compensatory damages against appellant out of the Real Estate Education, Research and Recovery Fund, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 10470-10483, and terminated appellant's license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent judgment creditor obtained judgment against appellant real estate broker for damages in a fraud action, which included an application for payment out of the Real Estate Education, Research and Recovery Fund (Fund) under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§10470-1048d. A hearing was held on the application with objector and respondent real estate commissioner. Appellant was present, but was prohibited by law from being a party to the hearing and defending himself on the application. The trial court ordered respondent real estate commissioner to pay the judgment to respondent judgment creditor, and automatically suspended appellant's license to sell real estate pursuant to § 10475. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed the award from the Fund to respondent judgment creditor, but vacated the order suspending appellant's license. The court noted that appellant was an aggrieved party within the meaning of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 938 for standing to appeal his license termination, and found that defendant was denied due process and that § 10475 was unconstitutional because it provided for an automatic suspension of the license of a real estate broker or salesman without a hearing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the automatic suspension of appellant real estate broker's license without a hearing because the applicable statute for the suspension was unconstitutional, as it prohibited by law a broker from being a party to the hearing and defending himself, thereby denying him due process.

 

1005 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re WILLIAM S.


Civ. No. 9959


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


10 Cal. App. 3d 944; 89 Cal. Rptr. 685; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1905



August 28, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, Richard L. Vaughn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order declaring the minor a ward, pursuant to section 601 of the Welfare and Institutions Code, and committing him to Rancho del Campo, is affirmed. The order changing the commitment from Rancho del Campo to the Youth Authority is reversed. The proceedings are remanded to the superior court, sitting as a juvenile court, for such further consideration as may be proper under the law and the facts in the premises.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant minor sought review of the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which committed him to a ranch that was a placement facility for wards of the juvenile court.


OVERVIEW: Respondent probation officer filed a petition that alleged appellant minor was a person within the provisions of Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §601. Respondent alleged that appellant had been using narcotics and dangerous drugs. A hearing was held before a referee who found all of the allegations to be true, ordered appellant declared a ward under the provisions of §601, and directed that he be committed to a ranch which was a placement facility for wards of the juvenile court. Five days after the ranch commitment, a supplemental petition was filed which said that competent medical authority stated that the minor would not adjust to the ordered program or rehabilitation. The referee found the allegations were true and ordered appellant committed to the California Youth Authority. Appellant filed a pro se appeal to each of the orders. The court reversed the referee because the commitment was never executed so the argument that the commitment was ineffective was without evidentiary support.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order declaring appellant minor a ward and committing him to the ranch. The court reversed the order changing appellant's commitment to the youth authority because the lower court was not authorized to commit appellant to the youth authority until it made an order for his treatment or commitment and found facts sufficient to support the conclusion that the previous disposition had not been effective.

 

1006 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Dale S.


Civ. No. 1294


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


10 Cal. App. 3d 952; 89 Cal. Rptr. 499; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1906



August 28, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 22, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, J. Kelly Steele, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile, claiming violations of U.S. Const. amend. V and XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 13, appealed the order of the juvenile judge in the Superior Court of Kern County (California), who, after conducting a hearing de novo on the parents' appeal under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 558 challenging the referee's order depriving them of custody of defendant, set aside the order and committed him to the California Youth Authority.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was adjudged a ward of the juvenile court under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602. The referee of the juvenile court ordered that defendant remain in the custody of the probation office. The juvenile judge, as required under Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 555, approved the referee's order as required because the order deprived the parents custody of their child. Defendant's parents filed an appeal under Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 558 and after a hearing de novo the juvenile judge set aside the referee's order. Defendant appealed and contended that the judge's order approving the referee's order was a final order and that U.S. Const. amend. V and XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 13 were violated because he was placed in double jeopardy in the second hearing. The court held that juveniles were entitled to the same constitutional protections as adults in criminal proceedings. The court held that a rehearing pursuant to an appeal filed by a minor or his parents under § 558 did not constitute a new proceeding on the same matter, and because the rehearing conducted by the juvenile judge was a part of the original proceeding, double jeopardy did not apply.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the juvenile judge's order commiting defendant juvenile to custody and held that there was no double jeopardy because the de novo hearing by the juvenile judge was a continuation of the original proceeding rather than a new proceeding to which double jeopardy would have attached.

 

1007 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dontanville


Crim. No. 16700


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


10 Cal. App. 3d 783; 89 Cal. Rptr. 172; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1889



August 24, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Carlos M. Teran and Howard D. McClain, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a review of the judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted defendant of a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288. Defendnant alleged that his right to a fair and speedy trial was violated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a sexual assault against a minor and he challenged his conviction alleging a denial of the right to a fair and speedy trial. The court affirmed defendant's conviction because the state's delay in filing the complaint was reasonable because defendant was on trial for a murder charge and a conviction in that case would have made the present action futile and it would have caused prejudicial publicity. The court held that defendant was not improperly denied counsel at his photographic lineup because the presence of counsel was not required. The court held that defendant's identification was not the result of the witnesses' exposure to an unfair assemblage of photographs because each child was called in separately to view the photographs, was admonished not to discuss what transpired with the others, and identified a number of other distinctive features which they remembered about defendant. The court held that given the victim's age, evidence that defendant touched the victim's stomach was sufficient to constitute a violation of Cal. Penal Code § 288.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction against defendant in a sexual assault case because the state's delay in bring the action was reasonable given that defendant was on trial in an unrelated murder charge. The court also held that defendant's photographic identification by a child was appropriate because defendant was not entitled to counsel at photographic lineup and lineup was not suggestive.

 

1008 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Ammons


Crim. No. 17527


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


10 Cal. App. 3d 682; 89 Cal. Rptr. 360; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1879



August 20, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 15, 1970. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, Edwin F. Beach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to count I and reversed as to count II.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant criminal defendant sought review of his conviction in the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), where a jury found him guilty of two counts of hit and run driving in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 20001. Appellant argued that § 20001 was unconstitutional because complying with it would have required him to incriminate himself.


OVERVIEW: Appellant criminal defendant argued that his conviction under Cal. Veh. Code § 20001 was unconstitutional because complying with the law would have required self-incrimination. Evidence at trial showed that appellant was in danger of prosecution for making an illegal left turn, driving while under the influence of alcohol, and driving after his operator's license had been revoked. The court held that appellant's right against self-incrimination excused him from the statutory requirement to identify himself at the scene of the accident. However, the court found no constitutional exemption from the other duties imposed by § 20001, including the duty to render reasonable assistance to any injured person. The court affirmed appellant's conviction under § 20001 for his failure to render assistance to one of the injured parties at the accident scene, holding that the nature of her injuries made the duty to render aid obvious. The court reversed appellant's conviction for his failure to aid the second injured party, because a reasonable jury could have concluded that the second party's injuries were minor enough that no duty to render aid existed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed appellant criminal defendant's conviction for failing to identify himself at the scene of the hit and run because it would have violated his right against self-incrimination. But the court held that appellant was not absolved from his statutory duty to provide reasonable assistance to injured persons and affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting him for failing to aid a seriously injured person at the accident scene.

 

1009 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ventura Realty Co. v. Robinson


Civ. No. 36163


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


10 Cal. App. 3d 628; 89 Cal. Rptr. 117; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1872



August 19, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 15, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, Robert R. Willard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought an appeal of the decision by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which entered a judgment of dismissal against plaintiffs for not amending their pleading within 10 days in an action against defendants, board of supervisors and county executive, to enjoin the construction of a new county civic center complex outside the town limits.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, board of supervisors and county executive, sought to construct a courthouse and other county offices on a site presently within the corporate limits of the City of San Buenaventura but outside the city limits as they existed on the date the seat of justice of Ventura County was established at the Town of San Buenaventura. Plaintiff taxpayers brought suit to enjoin the construction because defendants failed to secure the consent of two-thirds of the county's voters in an election as provided in Cal. Const. art. XI, § 2. The court affirmed the dismissal because it held that the erection and construction of a new courthouse on the site selected was not a removal of the county seat within the meaning of the language used in Cal. Const. art. XI, § 2. The court found that Cal. Gov't Code § 23600 provided that where a county seat was an incorporated city it included all annexed territory. Therefore, the court held that construction of a new courthouse did not require action by the electorate because the new courthouse site was within the incorporated territory of the present city and was at the county seat.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff taxpayer's action to enjoin construction by defendants, board of supervisors and county executive, of a new civic center outside the town limits because the county seat included all annexed territory and the county seat was located in an incorporated city.

 

1010 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Stuller


Crim. No. 4001


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


10 Cal. App. 3d 582; 89 Cal. Rptr. 158; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1868; 41 A.L.R.3d 712



August 14, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 9, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, Richard M. Marsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions of forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261(3) and oral copulation of a 14-year-old child under Cal. Penal Code § 288a, entered in the Superior Court of Riverside County (California); defendant challenged the constitutionality of Palm Springs, Cal., Ordinance Code art. 2942, a bartender registration ordinance and asserted various procedural and evidentiary error at his trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant committed sex crimes against two teenage girls. During the course of one of the incidents, he left fingerprints; he also dropped a piece of paper on which he had written a bogus note to the manager of the girl's apartment building. A few days later, he went to the police station voluntarily to register as a bartender under Palm Springs, Cal., Ordinance Code art. 2942.Writing samples and fingerprints taken from his application played a critical role in his convictions for forcible rape under Cal. Penal Code § 261(3) and oral copulation of a 14-year-old child under Cal. Penal Code § 288a. The court affirmed, finding that the ordinance was a valid exercise of the municipality's police power. The information on the application, basic information such as defendant's telephone number and address, was readily ascertainable, and fingerprints and handwriting samples were outside the scope of the protections against self-incrimination in U.S. Const. amend. V. Likewise, defendant's claims relating to self-incrimination, privacy, discrimination, the right of employment, and search and seizure were rejected, as were challenges to certain procedural and evidentiary rulings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, rejecting his claim that a bartender registration ordinance with which he voluntarily complied was unconstitutional. Handwriting samples and fingerprints taken from the application became critical evidence in the prosecution's case against defendant.

 

1011 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Montez v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 36021


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


10 Cal. App. 3d 343; 88 Cal. Rptr. 736; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1845



August 7, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The alternative writ is discharged. Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue prohibiting the respondent court from any further proceedings in the case entitled People of the State of California v. Carlos Montez et al., being its number A-244906, without first reopening the hearing on the petitioners' motion to quash the indictment and proceeding in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion or, alternatively, in its discretion, proceeding to hear said motion de novo.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied their motion to quash felony indictments. Petitioners alleged that the grand jury was illegally constituted in that eligible jurors of the class to which petitioners belonged were systematically excluded from consideration for grand jury nomination.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners moved to quash felony indictments on the ground that Spanish-named Mexican Americans were systematically excluded from consideration for grand jury nomination. The trial court announced that it would not permit petitioners to interrogate 70 grand jury selector judges as witnesses unless petitioners could show statistical evidence of discrimination in grand jury nominations for the year in which the indictments were handed down. Statistics showed that Mexican Americans were better represented than they had been in preceding years, therefore the trial court held that petitioners did not prove a prima facie case of discrimination, and the motion was denied. The court held that roughly proportionate representation did not equate lack of discrimination. The court determined that an offer of proof was not required in petitioners' case, and that refusal to permit petitioners to examine the subpoenaed judges was erroneous under Cal. Evid. Code § 354. Accordingly the court held a peremptory writ of prohibition was proper. The court was instructed to refrain from further proceedings without reopening the hearing on petitioners' motion or hearing the motion de novo.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of prohibition and instructed the trial court to refrain from further proceedings without opening the hearing on petitioners' motion to quash, or to hear the motion de novo because the court held that petitioners were erroneously precluded from producing grand jury selector judges as their own witnesses as statistical evidence did not conclusively disprove discrimination.

 

1012 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Fletcher v. Western National Life Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 9281


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


10 Cal. App. 3d 376; 89 Cal. Rptr. 78; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1847; 47 A.L.R.3d 286



August 7, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 27, 1970, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 1, 1970. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, William S. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insurer appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that found after jury trial for plaintiff insured on plaintiff's claim for intentional infliction of emotional distress arising from bad faith conduct involving disability insurance policy.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff insured was injured on the job and filed a claim with defendant disability insurer. Defendant refused to pay benefits under the injury provisions of a policy, accused plaintiff of misrepresentation, demanded return of benefits paid under sickness provision, and impliedly threatened expensive litigation. Holding that the evidence established that defendant's concededly "outrageous and deplorable" conduct had caused plaintiff severe emotional distress, the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that physical symptoms of distress were not required and that it was the jury's province to determine whether the distress was severe. In addition, the court held that threatened and actual bad faith refusals to make payments under a policy, maliciously employed in concert with false and threatening communications directed to its insured for the purpose of causing him to surrender his policy or disadvantageously settle a nonexistent dispute on the part of a disability insurer, gave rise to an independent action for tortious interference with a protected property interest for which all proximately caused damages, including economic damages, and punitive damages, were recoverable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment for plaintiff insured because sufficient evidence existed to submit to the jury the issue of whether defendant insurer's bad faith refusal to pay, accusations of misrepresentation, and demands for return of benefits caused plaintiff severe emotional distress, and defendant's actions gave rise to separate cause of tortious interference with protected property interest.

 

1013 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brown


Crim. No. 3736


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


10 Cal. App. 3d 169; 88 Cal. Rptr. 801; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1828



July 31, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 24, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Eli H. Levenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted appellant of contributing to the delinquency of her minor daughters. Appellant's motion for a new trial was denied, and her application for probation was eventually withdrawn. The trial court sentenced her to serve 90 days in the county jail and ordered her to pay a fine.


OVERVIEW: After a trial, a jury was unable to agree on a burglary charge, but found appellant guilty of contributing to the delinquency of her minor daughters. Appellant contended that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to hear, determine, and sentence on the misdemeanor charge, because the jury failed to arrive at a verdict on the felony charge. While conceding related felony and misdemeanor counts could be alleged in the same information, appellant maintained that the trial court's jurisdiction to hear and determine the misdemeanor charge was dependent upon, and lacking without, a conviction of the felony charge. The court affirmed the conviction. The question of whether the two offenses charged were sufficiently related to permit their joinder in the same information was a matter for the court to decide and not a question for jury determination. The court's review of the evidence indicated that all the evidence introduced on the misdemeanor charge had to do with appellant's activities in the market. The same evidence was directly related to the charge of burglary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which convicted appellant of contributing to the delinquency of her minor daughters. The court's review of the evidence indicated that all the evidence introduced on the misdemeanor charge had to do with appellant's activities in the market, and that joinder of a felony charge, for which she was not convicted, allowed the trial court jurisdiction over both charges.

 

1014 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Fink v. Goodson-Todman Enterprises, Ltd.


Civ. No. 33731


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


9 Cal. App. 3d 996; 88 Cal. Rptr. 679; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2013; 169 U.S.P.Q. (BNA) 106



July 27, 1970  
July 27, 1970


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 24, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Walter C. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed as to count 3 of the complaint; it is reversed as to all other counts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, who proposed a television program to defendant producers, appealed a dismissal judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) following the sustaining of a general demurrer interposed by defendants on several counts alleging, inter alia, breach of express and implied contract, and breach of fiduciary duty.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff who proposed a television program to defendant producers appealed from a dismissal judgment following the sustaining of a general demurrer interposed by defendants to several counts. Plaintiff alleged that defendants had taken his presentation and broadcast a series based on it without his consent. Among the counts were counts for breach of an express contract, breach of implied contract, breach of defendants' fiduciary (quasi-contractual) duty, infringement of plaintiff's common law copyright interest, and fraudulent preclusion. The court affirmed as to the breach of fiduciary count but reversed as to all other counts. Plaintiff contended that the trial court erred in finding that there was no similarity between the respective works. The court agreed, finding that upon looking through defendants' production a "spine" of structural dimensions similar to plaintiff's material existed. In particular, the basic themes were strikingly similar and the back stories were substantially similar.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as to the count for breach of defendants' fiduciary duty, but was reversed as to all other counts since substantial similarity between the two works was evidenced by the basic theme and story similarities.

 

1015 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Igna v. City of Baldwin Park


Civ. No. 35539


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


9 Cal. App. 3d 909; 88 Cal. Rptr. 581; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2003



July 23, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, A. J. Bernhardt, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, as amended nunc pro tunc, is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trailer park owner appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which dismissed her action against defendant city for damages by way of inverse condemnation, injunctive and declaratory relief, and for mandamus to compel defendant to issue improvement permits on the ground that defendant unconstitutionally applied zoning ordinance to plaintiff's property.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff trailer park owner, in business before the incorporation of defendant city, applied to defendant for improvement permits. Defendant informed plaintiff that the trailer parks, in light of recent zoning ordinances, became nonconforming uses absent a conditional use permit issued by defendant after application therefor. Instead of applying for the conditional use permit, plaintiff filed an action for mandamus to compel defendant to issue the improvement permits, injunctive and declaratory relief, and for damages by way of inverse condemnation. The trial court sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend and dismissed plaintiff's action. The court affirmed. The court held that plaintiff failed to exhaust the administrative remedies provided by Baldwin Park, California Municipal Code § 9474 et seq. Therefore, the court properly sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend because plaintiff failed to state a cause of action. The court found that defendant's zoning ordinance applied retroactively because plaintiff did not engage in substantial building or incur expenses in connection with the proposed improvements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendant city in plaintiff trailer park owner's action for mandamus to compel defendant to issue improvement permits because plaintiff failed to exhaust administrative remedies provided in zoning ordinance. Therefore, the court held that plaintiff failed to state a cause of action, and the trial court properly sustained defendant's demurrer without leave to amend.

 

1016 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Baby Boy T.


Civ. No. 35086


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


9 Cal. App. 3d 815; 88 Cal. Rptr. 418; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1992



July 22, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Lester E. Olson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order and judgment declaring the minor person free from the custody and control of his mother is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother of minor sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which declared the minor free from the custody and control of appellant in an action by respondent Department of Adoptions of County of Los Angeles pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g). Appellant claimed that Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g) was unconstitutionally vague because the statute did not define mental deficiency or mental illness.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Department of Adoptions of County of Los Angeles filed a petition against appellant mother of minor pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g) to have the minor declared free from the custody of appellant. On appeal, appellant contended that Cal. Civ. Code § 232(g) was unconstitutionally vague because it did not contain a definition of a mental deficiency or mental illness. The court rejected this contention and affirmed the decision. The court found that Cal. Civ. Code 232(g) was not unconstitutionally vague because in enacting the statute, it was the legislative intent to embody the definition of mental deficiency then set forth in Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 5550.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment declaring the minor free from the custody and control of appellant mother of minor in respondent Department of Adoptions of County of Los Angeles' action. The court found that the applicable statute authorizing a petition to free a minor from the custody and control of a parent was not unconstitutionally vague because the definition of mental deficiency was contained in another statute.

 

1017 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Castro v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 34178


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


9 Cal. App. 3d 675; 88 Cal. Rptr. 500; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1985



July 17, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining the respondent court from further prosecution of counts XV and XVI of the indictment. With respect to count VIII the petition for a writ of prohibition is denied and the superior court is directed to proceed in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner demonstrators sought writ of prohibition against respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), restraining it from proceeding on conspiracy, and other crimes against them. Petitioners claimed the indictments were defective because they were not supported by direct evidence, the grand jury was constituted unlawfully, and they were lawfully exercising their constitutional rights at the time of arrest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners student-demonstrators of Hispanic heritage organized a mass demonstration and some disturbances occurred; petitioners were indicted. The prosecution attempted to prove the conspiracy charge using hearsay testimony of alleged co-conspirators. Petitioners claimed that unless prima facie proof was first produced the court should not allow hearsay statements to prove the charge. The court agreed with petitioners and held that the conspiracy indictment was defective because there was no direct evidence to support the charge and petitioners had no knowledge that disturbances would occur. It also held that a demonstration was a constitutionally guaranteed activity under U.S. Const. amend. I., so something more than mere hearsay was required to prove the conspiracy because petitioners had a right to assemble. Petitioners also argued that the co-conspirator's statements should not be used unless the prosecution first proved a prima facie case. The court granted the writ of prohibition stating that hearsay was improper because stricter scrutiny had to be used when attempting to quash constitutionally protected activities and prima facie proof of conspiracy must first be shown.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner demonstrators' writ of prohibition on both the conspiracy and other misdemeanor charges. It restrained further prosecution on those counts because petitioners had a First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, right to assemble and demonstrate. It held that no direct testimony proved that any misbehavior was planned, or that petitioners knew in advance that crimes would be committed, so conspiracy charge was improper.

 

1018 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Carlson


Civ. No. 12225


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


9 Cal. App. 3d 479; 88 Cal. Rptr. 229; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1964; 41 A.L.R.3d 825



July 8, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 3, 1970. Peters, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, William A. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Socialist Labor Party of California sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which found that respondent state was the sole heir of an estate, in proceedings instituted by petitioner executor to determine heirship. Appellant had been named beneficiary under the will.


OVERVIEW: A will designated appellant Socialist Labor Party of California as sole primary beneficiary. The will designated respondent state as the sole beneficiary if appellant was unable to take the estate for any reason, legal or otherwise. The probate court held in favor of respondent, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed and held that appellant, as a political party, was not entitled to receive a testamentary gift. Appellant was not a religious, benevolent or fraternal unincorporated association, or a labor organization, and thus not a type of organization specifically authorized by Cal. Prob. Code § 27, or Cal. Corp. Code § 21200, to receive testamentary gifts. If courts conferred the will-making power upon unincorporated political parties, the judicial will-making power would seem almost limitless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding respondent state to be the sole heir of an estate because appellant Socialist Labor Party of California, as a political party, was not a type of association statutorily allowed to receive testamentary gifts.

 

1019 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


City of San Joaquin v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 1020


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


9 Cal. App. 3d 365; 88 Cal. Rptr. 12; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1954



July 1, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, Harold V. Thompson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city and defendant board of equalization both sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which found that a certain company was not a construction contractor under the Bradley-Burns Uniform Local Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 7201 et seq., and that the procedure adopted by defendant for the allocation of sales tax revenues was proper and did not deprive plaintiff of tax revenues.


OVERVIEW: After plaintiff city adopted a sales tax ordinance under the Bradley-Burns Uniform Local Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal Rev. & Tax. Code § 7200 et seq., it contracted with defendant board of equalization for administration of the ordinance and collection of the sales taxes. Under a new administrative ruling, defendant began classifying pumps sold by a certain company as fixtures, which resulted in the reclassifying of the sales tax and a decrease in the amount received by plaintiff on the pump sales. Consequently, plaintiff filed a declaratory action challenging defendant's power to classify the pumps as fixtures, and attacking the legality of the "pooling procedure" used by defendant in allocating revenues from construction contracts. The superior court found that the pump company was not a contractor under the regulation, and that the pumps were not fixtures, but also ruled that the procedure adopted by defendant for the allocation of tax revenues was proper. Both parties appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed, concluding that the judgment as to the company was proper, and that defendant's pooling procedure was an accounting system rather than a regulation subject to interpretation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, finding that the company at issue was engaged only in the business of installing machinery and equipment that did not improve real property, and thus it was taxable by plaintiff city. The court also agreed with the superior court that the pooling procedure used by defendant board of equalization was not a regulation, but an accounting system that did not unconstitutionally deprive plaintiff of its tax revenues.

 

1020 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Steven C.


Civ. No. 26819


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


9 Cal. App. 3d 255; 88 Cal. Rptr. 97; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1943



June 29, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 26, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, Raymond J. O'Connor, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which adjudicated him a ward of the juvenile court and committed him to the youth authority, having found that he violated Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), by assaulting the principal of his school with a firearm. Defendant contended, inter alia, that he was denied due process, and denied his right to a jury trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant juvenile was found to have violated Cal. Penal Code § 245(a), assault with a deadly weapon or instrument or by any means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, after the principal of defendant's high school was shot at through the walls of his office during the school day. Defendant was adjudicated a ward of the juvenile court and committed to the California Youth Authority, and appealed the findings of the juvenile court, contending that he was not properly given Miranda warnings before being questioned, that he was denied his right to a jury trial, that the evidence was insufficient to sustain the finding that he violated § 245(a), and that he was denied due process because the court adjudicated his guilt on the basis of a preponderance of the evidence standard, rather than by proof beyond a reasonable doubt. On appeal, the court found meritless most of defendant's contentions, but reversed the juvenile court's judgment based upon the adjudication by preponderance of the evidence, finding that proof of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt was a U.S. Const. amend. XIV due process requirement applicable in juvenile proceedings. The case was remanded for a re-trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the juvenile court, finding that although juveniles had no right to a jury trial, defendant was denied due process in that he was found to have violated the statute under a preponderance of the evidence standard, rather than beyond a reasonable doubt. The court also found that defendant knowingly waived his Miranda rights. The court remanded the case for a re-trial on the facts under the proper standard.

Monica Theater v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 33606


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


9 Cal. App. 3d 1; 88 Cal. Rptr. 71; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1922



June 23, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 8, 1970, and the petition of the plaintiffs and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 11, 1970. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Beach Vasey, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff theater owner sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a writ of mandate, and held respondent Municipal Court for the Beverly Hills Judicial District of Los Angeles properly held that an affidavit sufficiently established probable cause, and plaintiff's film and photos were properly seized, and that respondent correctly ruled that plaintiff was not entitled to the seized items.


OVERVIEW: After viewing a film and photographs at appellant theater owner's business, a vice-officer executed an affidavit for a seizure warrant for allegedly obscene material. Respondent municipal court issued a warrant without viewing the film, and the officer seized the photographs and one copy of the film. In the misdemeanor proceeding, plaintiff filed a motion to suppress and return of the seized properties. During a probable cause hearing, a magistrate viewed the film, photographs, and comparison films, and held that the warrant was issued properly. Plaintiff filed for a writ of mandate alleging that respondent had not viewed the film prior to the warrant and had not conducted a pre-warrant adversary hearing. The trial court denied the writ, concluding that the affidavit sufficiently established probable cause that the materials were obscene, that they were properly seized, and that plaintiff was not entitled to restoration. The court affirmed the denial of the writ because respondent conducted a Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 proceeding subsequent to the issuance of the warrant, providing ample constitutional protection, and held that a pre-warrant adversarial hearing was not required.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of mandate, and held that plaintiff theater owner's film and photographs were properly seized, because a pre-warrant adversarial proceeding by respondent municipal court was not required for seizure of allegedly obscene material due to the fact that said hearing was provided post-seizure. The court also held that existing state procedures provided ample constitutional protection.

 

1022 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Horner


Crim. No. 16870


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


9 Cal. App. 3d 23; 87 Cal. Rptr. 917; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 1923



June 23, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Francis X. Marnell, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), following his conviction of violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12420, and the subsequent order of the superior court denying his motion for a new trial. The statute made it a crime to possess a shell, cartridge, or bomb capable of emitting tear gas.


OVERVIEW: After stopping the car defendant was driving, in response to a citizen's complaint of being threatened, officers observed a B.B. gun in the car and placed defendant under arrest. He consented to a search of the car, during which police discovered a canister defendant later stated contained tear gas. At the preliminary hearing, the state's chemist confirmed the substance was tear gas. In affirming the judgment, the court rejected the argument that Cal. Penal Code §§ 12420 and 12421 were unconstitutionally vague, finding irrelevant the hypothetical situations posed by defendant. The court declined to declare the statutes unconstitutional based on hypothetical situations when they clearly covered the facts of defendant's case. It also rejected defendant's claim that submission of his case on the preliminary transcript was made without his effective consent because the record showed that he was fully advised of his rights, and chose to waive them. Where defendant produced no exculpatory evidence, the court refused to find that defense counsel's submission of the case on the preliminary transcript amounted to a farce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment because the statute which defendant was convicted of violating was not unconstitutionally vague; the facts of his case were clearly covered by it, and he was not denied due process in the proceedings leading up to his conviction.

 

1023 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Pearce


Crim. No. 16816


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


8 Cal. App. 3d 984; 87 Cal. Rptr. 814; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2113



June 19, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 12, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Adolph Alexander, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), of possession of marijuana, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530, and sale of heroin, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501. Defendant, who was accused by indictment, argued that constitutional due process was violated because he was deprived of rights that would have been afforded to him if he had been prosecuted after a hearing before a magistrate.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530 and sale of heroin in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11501. Defendant appealed his convictions, but the court affirmed the judgments and rejected defendant's arguments that his prosecution by process of grand jury indictment deprived him of due process under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court pointed out that the safeguard of the equal protection clause is equality under the law, rather than identifying rights and privileges. The court reasoned that the state's decision to prosecute certain crimes through the use of a grand jury did not deny defendant equal protection, though the grand jury indictment and the preliminary hearing information procedures were different in form, because the constitution permitted such classifications that were rationally related to the state's legitimate ends.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of possession of marijuana and sale of heroin. The court held that defendant's due process rights were not violated because he was indicted by a grand jury, as defendant was not treated unequally in comparison to persons who were equally situated, and defendant had not made any showing of intentional or purposeful discrimination against him.

Herick v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 34072


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


8 Cal. App. 3d 967; 87 Cal. Rptr. 646; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2111



June 18, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, A. Curtis Smith, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairman of of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for writ of mandate to compel the municipal court to provide him with a free reporter's transcript of the proceedings in which he was convicted of disturbing the peace.


OVERVIEW: The municipal court denied appellant's motion to order preparation of a free reporter's transcript of proceedings convicting him of misdemeanor disturbance of the peace. The municipal court gave appellant a 15-day extension to procure a court settled statement on appeal however appellant made no effort to do so. Appellant then sought a petition of mandate from the superior court to compel the municipal court to provide him with the transcript on the basis that he was indigent and that the transcript was essential for adequate presentation of his appeal. The motion was denied and appellant sought review. The court held that an attempt to secure a settled statement was a prerequisite to the ripening of defendant's right to a free transcript. The court also held that appellant was present at trial and therefore should be able to signify how materials not included in the transcript would be of use to him on appeal. Therefore the court held that although a non-indigent could purchase a transcript without reason, the requirement that an indigent defendant proceed first by way of a settled statement did not constitute invidious discrimination. Accordingly the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed because an attempt to secure a settled statement was a prerequisite to the ripening of defendant's right to a free transcript and the record did not show that an attempt was made. Additionally appellant did not show that that the prerequisite violated his equal protection rights.

 

1025 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Martin v. County of Contra Costa


Civ. No. 26358


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


8 Cal. App. 3d 856; 87 Cal. Rptr. 886; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2100



June 17, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, S. C. Masterson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings against the appropriate county officers in conformance with the views expressed in this opinion. Let each side bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, county and board of supervisors, appealed from a judgment by the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which directed the issuance of a peremptory writ of mandate commanding them to compensate and provide benefits for plaintiff municipal court employees, equal to and with the same effective date as other county employees, and awarding plaintiffs back uniform allowances.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff municipal court employees obtained a writ of mandate commanding respondents, county and board of supervisors, to compensate and provide benefits to them equivalent to those of other county employees and awarding them back uniform allowances. Defendants appealed. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 73349, which required subsequent changes in benefits to apply equally to employees of the municipal courts and marshals' offices, was not an unlawful delegation of legislative power and did not violate Cal. Const. art. VI, § 5. The uniform allowance granted to deputy sheriffs should have applied to plaintiffs, who were deputy marshals, pursuant to § 73349 because they were entitled to the same compensation under the salary ordinance. However, the trial court's ruling impinged upon the legislative power of respondent board of supervisors because it compelled them to perform a legislative act. Because plaintiffs failed to join the ministerial county officers who were responsible for the administrative steps through which the compensation would be paid, the court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause for further proceedings against those county officials.


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of plaintiff municipal court employees, which directed defendants, county and board of supervisors, to compensate and provide benefits for plaintiffs equal to those of other county employees and to pay back uniform allowances, was reversed because plaintiffs failed to join the proper ministerial officers of the county government, and the cause was remanded for proceedings against those county officers.

Zuckerman-Mandeville, Inc. v. Sheffield


Civ. No. 12118


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


8 Cal. App. 3d 793; 88 Cal. Rptr. 57; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2095



June 15, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 12, 1970.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Stanley Arnold, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Counsel.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant department of human resources development, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which held that Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 627(b) was unconstitutional as applied to plaintiff farmer in an action for a refund of unemployment insurance taxes paid under protest by plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sustained plaintiff farmer's position that defendant department of human resources development's denial of the agricultural exemption was a violation of the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and the uniformity and privileges and immunities provisions of Cal. Const. art. I, §§ 11, 21. The court found that all the employees involved were either clerical personnel or corporate officers whose duties were performed in the office off the ranch. The court found that there was no reason why the clerical and management personnel of plaintiff should not fall under the protective cloak of the Unemployment Insurance Act and that there should be no administrative or accounting difficulty in carrying out the requirements of the act. The court reversed and held the restriction of the agricultural labor exemption to services performed on the farm did not result in an unconstitutional discrimination against plaintiff who could not satisfy the requirements of the agricultural exemption.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the agricultural exemption for unemployment insurance taxes was constitutional as applied to plaintiff farmer.

State Bd. of Barber Examiners v. Star


Civ. No. 9599


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


8 Cal. App. 3d 736; 87 Cal. Rptr. 450; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2086



June 12, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, Ronald M. Crookshank, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff barber board sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied plaintiff's application for a preliminary injunction to prevent defendant barber shop from charging less than plaintiff's minimum price schedule for haircuts.


OVERVIEW: Defendant barber shop began charging less than plaintiff barber board's minimum price schedule for haircuts and plaintiff applied for a preliminary injunction pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6558. The court denied the application upon the constitutional ground that legislation providing for price fixing in the barber industry bears no reasonable relationship to the protection of the health and welfare of the people and plaintiff sought review. The court affirmed the denial of the injunction because the purpose of an injunction is to maintain the status quo of the parties pending the litigation and the status quo for the parties was defendant operating a barber shop charging less than plaintiff's minimum price schedule. The court held that the grant of the injunction would impose a hardship upon defendant. While the lower court purported to rule on a constitutional ground, the court held that was an interim ruling which will not bind the court one way or the other at the trial.


OUTCOME: The order which denied plaintiff barber board's application for preliminary injunction was affirmed because the parties were already at status quo and the injunction would impose a hardship on defendant barber shop.

 

1028 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kirby v. Superior Court


Crim. No. 35719


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


8 Cal. App. 3d 591; 87 Cal. Rptr. 577; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2068



June 10, 1970 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for a writ of prohibition is denied; the alternative writ of mandate is discharged. Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue, directing the respondent court: (1) to vacate its order denying petitioner's motion to suppress evidence; and (2) to hold a full evidentiary hearing, pursuant to such motion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5, petitioner requested a writ of prohibition or mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate its order denying his motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: A police officer saw a van parked in a manufacturing area of town. Because it was unusual to see a vehicle parked there at that time of day, and because there had been quite a few recent burglaries in the area, the officer decided to investigate. He checked the van for his own safety before checking the buildings because he was working alone. The officer opened the door to the van and pulled back the curtains. He saw petitioner and a nude 14-year-old boy. The officer separated the two and talked to each of them. Petitioner made an unsuccessful motion to suppress the officer's observations under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5(a); the trial court held that the officer's observations could not be challenged as a "tangible or intangible" thing under that statute. The court granted petitioner's request for a writ of mandate, directing the trial court to conduct a full evidentiary hearing on petitioner's motion. The admissibility of the evidence could properly be raised by a motion under § 1538.5. That section was intended to permit the challenge of any evidence obtained in violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV, including things with no physical form or substance.


OUTCOME: The court granted a peremptory writ of mandate and remanded the case for a full hearing on petitioner's motion to suppress. The trial court erred in holding that petitioner could not seek suppression of the observations of a police officer because that type of evidence was not intangible evidence subject to suppression under statutory procedure governing the suppression of tangible or intangible things.

 

1029 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Newton


Crim. No. 7753


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


8 Cal. App. 3d 359; 87 Cal. Rptr. 394; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2046



May 29, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On June 26, 1970, the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 29, 1970. McComb, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Monroe Friedman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which convicted him of involuntary manslaughter in the shooting of a police officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for voluntary manslaughter. Defendant was pulled over by two police officers. Gunfire erupted soon afterwards. One of the policemen was killed. Defendant denied shooting the officer and claimed he was unsure of what happened because he was unconscious after he was shot. The court reversed the conviction, holding that the trial court committed reversible error when it failed to instruct the jury as to defendant's involuntary, unconscious state. Involuntary unconsciousness provided a complete defense to a particular crime because it negated capacity to commit any crime. Defendant's evidence warranted an instruction concerning unconsciousness. The fact that such evidence might not have been wholly believable was no excuse for a failure to instruct. The error to instruct was prejudicial per se because it deprived defendant of his constitutional right to have the jury determine all material issues presented by evidence. Despite defendant's failure to request an instruction, the trial court was under a duty to instruct anyway where evidence existed concerning unconsciousness.


OUTCOME: The court reversed trial court's decision, finding that the trial court committed reversible error when it failed to instruct the jury on defendant's evidence of involuntary unconsciousness because it deprived defendant of his constitutional right to have jury determine all material issues presented by the evidence.

Estate of Yush


Civ. No. 35555


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


8 Cal. App. 3d 251; 87 Cal. Rptr. 222; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2036



May 28, 1970 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Arthur K. Marshall, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state controller challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which held that under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 14101 respondent estate administrator was liable for inheritance tax, and respondent beneficiary was responsible for a tax on a transfer outside probate.


OVERVIEW: Respondent estate administrator sought a final accounting of an estate in probate court. Appellant state controller objected because respondent beneficiary owed taxes on a transfer out of probate, although the estate owed no taxes. The trial court held that respondent administrator was liable for any inheritance taxed owed, under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 14101, and that respondent beneficiary owed taxes outside of probate. Appellant challenged, contending that the trial court's order for distribution was improper under Cal. Prob. Code § 10224 because all inheritance taxes had not been paid. The court affirmed the trial court's order settling the account. The court held that Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 14143 and Cal. Prob. Code § 1024 applied only to taxes owed upon or that were payable out of the property of the estate and did not apply to those taxes owed by reason of transfers of property outside of probate. The court reasoned that to have delayed distribution of estate proceeds would not have served any conceivable purpose.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial's court's decision to settle a probate account, holding that an estate administrator was not required to settle non-estate tax debts in order to close a probate estate.

City of San Diego v. American Federation of State Etc. Employees


Civ. No. 9952


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


8 Cal. App. 3d 308; 87 Cal. Rptr. 258; 1970 Cal. App. LEXIS 2043



May 28, 1970 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 24, 1970. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, Hugo M. Fisher, Judge.



DISPOSITION: That part of the order appealed from is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city sought review of part of an order of the Superior Court, San Diego County (California), which denied its application for a temporary injunction restraining defendants, union and related others, from engaging in a strike or work stoppage.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city had sought a temporary injunction to restrain defendants, union and others, from engaging in a strike or work stoppage. The trial court entered an order which denied plaintiff's temporary injunction on the ground that public employees could lawfully strike. Plaintiff appealed the order of the trial court. The court reversed the portion of the order denying the injunction. The court held that the evidence would have supported an order granting a preliminary injunction and that the trial court did not exercise its discretion in the premises. The trial court's decision was the exclusive product of an erroneous concept of the law. The court held that, absent an authorizing statute, a public employee had no right to strike.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court to the extent that it denied plaintiff city's request for a temporary injunction restraining defendants, union and related others, from engaging in a strike or work stoppage. The court held that, absent an authorizing statute, a public employee had no right to strike.
