In re R.


Civ. No. 9586


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


274 Cal. App. 2d 749; 79 Cal. Rptr. 247; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2108



July 11, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from orders of the Superior Court of Orange County committing a minor to a juvenile home. James F. Judge and Bruce W. Sumner, Judges.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a minor, appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that committed him to a juvenile home.


OVERVIEW: Defendant admitted selling seven "lids" of marijuana on his high school campus. He also confessed that he sold marijuana on other occasions, as well. A dispositional order was entered committing defendant to a juvenile home for 60 days. On appeal, the court reversed the order. The court first found that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 601 was not unconstitutionally vague and indefinite. The court then concluded that the superior court did not err when it admitted defendant's statements because they were made after administration of the Miranda warnings and were not the result of an illegal detention. Further, the court ruled that there was sufficient evidence to indicate that defendant was in danger of leading a dissolute life, for purposes of § 601. However, because the proof of the allegation rested solely upon the admission of the minor, the order was reversed. Defendant properly contended that proof of the commission of the drug-related felony was addressed under the same standards as a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602. An informer's tip constituted inadmissible hearsay.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order.

 

1103 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Stults v. Thompson


Civ. No. 1078


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


274 Cal. App. 2d 733; 79 Cal. Rptr. 520; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2106



July 10, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied August 7, 1969, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 4, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Kern County. Walter Osborn, Jr., Judge.

Action to quiet title to real property.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgments of dismissal affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff administrator challenged an order from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) that dismissed his action to quiet title for failure to bring the action to trial within five years after the complaint was filed pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 583.


OVERVIEW: The administrator's decedent filed a complaint against one defendant to quiet title in certain real property and later amended the complaint to allege additional causes of action and to name additional defendants. The decedent was granted an indefinite continuance before she died, and after her death, the administrator was named as a substitute party. After the statutory five-year period elapsed, the trial court granted defendants' motion to dismiss the action for lack of prosecution, and on appeal, the court affirmed, holding that there was no ground upon which to find an implied exception to § 583. Filing an amended complaint, either to state a new cause of action or to bring in new parties, did not extend the § 583 requirement that the action must be brought to trial within five years. The five year period commenced from the date of the original filing because all causes of action alleged by plaintiff had accrued at the time of the filing of the original complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order that dismissed the administrator's complaint seeking to quiet title.

Watson v. County of Merced


Civ. No. 1091


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


274 Cal. App. 2d 263; 78 Cal. Rptr. 807; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2047



June 24, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Merced County. Donald R. Fretz, Judge.

Action by mobilehome owners against county to have ordinance imposing license fee on mobilehomes declared unconstitutional.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for defendants affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owners of mobile homes appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Merced County (California) declaring that an ordinance imposing a license fee on mobile homes not located in mobile home parks and outside of unincorporated areas did not violate Cal. Const. art. XI, § 11, entered in the owners' action to have defendant county's ordinance declared unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The owners challenged a county ordinance imposing a license fee on mobile homes. They contended that the ordinance was unconstitutional because the state had preempted the field. They also contended that the proposed fee was too high. The trial court entered a judgment that the ordinance was not invalid. On appeal, the court held that the facts and the law supported the findings and judgment of the trial court. Although generally the state had taken over the question of taxing mobile homes and regulating their major uses, the legislature had specifically authorized the action taken by the county ordinance. As for the amount of the fee, the presumption was in favor of its reasonableness. That the county had not fully regulated the licensing of mobile homes or had set a fee that was inadequate to cover the expenses of regulation did not prevent it from increasing its fees for regulation to a reasonable amount when it had jurisdiction to control the fee.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment upholding the county ordinance and the license fee imposed by the county.

Merco Constr. Engineers, Inc. v. Los Angeles Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. 32044


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


274 Cal. App. 2d 154; 79 Cal. Rptr. 23; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2035



June 19, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 13, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Bayard Rhone, Judge.

Action against a school district for assessing an allegedly illegal penalty against certain sums due for the construction of a high school.



DISPOSITION: Reversed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to amended complaint sustained without leave to amend reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractor brought an action against defendant school district and claimed that the school district acted illegally in assessing a $ 77,000 penalty against certain sums due plaintiff for the construction of a high school. The contractor appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained the school district's general demurrer to the contractor's amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, a licensed general contractor, submitted a bid for the construction of a high school. In the preliminary compilation of its bid, the contractor included the cost of mechanical work from a subcontractor. The contractor subsequently found a bid that was $ 41,500 less than the other subcontractor's bid and reduced its total bid by that sum. The contractor later discovered that the subcontractor's bid on which it relied did not include the automatic fire sprinkler system and that the reasonable cost of furnishing and installing such a system was $ 88,500. Exercising its discretion under Cal. Gov't Code § 4110, the school district assessed a penalty of $ 77,000 against the contractor. This sum was equal to 10 percent of the subcontractor's bid and it was to be deducted from the amount earned by the contractor in performance of the prime contract. The trial court sustained the school district's general demurrer. The court noted that the penalty assessed was the monetary maximum of $ 77,000. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code § 4110 was unconstitutional because it failed to provide the contractor with notice and a hearing prior to the imposition of the penalty.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1106 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Seale


Crim. No. 7428


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


274 Cal. App. 2d 107; 78 Cal. Rptr. 811; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2029



June 18, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied September 17, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County granting probation in a prosecution for knowingly bringing a firearm onto grounds adjacent to a city jail. Redmond C. Staats, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that granted probation in a prosecution for knowingly bringing a firearm onto grounds adjacent to a city jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was observed sitting on a retaining wall located near the city jail, holding a shotgun. He was convicted of the Cal. Penal Code § 4574 offense, and the judgment was affirmed on appeal. The court first held that there was sufficient evidence to support the finding that defendant knew that he was on grounds adjacent to a jail, for purposes of § 4574. Defendant was only three feet away from the doorway that was inscribed with the name of the jail. The inferences logically drawn from the facts supported the conclusion that he knew he was near the jail. The court then rejected the claim that the prosecution failed to prove that defendant had the specific intent to facilitate an inmate's escape from the jail. Specific intent was not an element of § 4574, but rather of Cal. Penal Code § 4535, with which defendant was not charged. The court further ruled that defendant's right to equal protection was not denied by the prosecution's decision not to charge him with § 4535. Finally, the court held that defendant was not shielded by the Second Amendment from prosecution under State weapons possession laws and that the offense encompassed acts performed outside of the jail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

1107 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Hickey v. Roby


Civ. No. 25534


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


273 Cal. App. 2d 752; 77 Cal. Rptr. 486; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2224



June 9, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 6, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco setting aside an injunction. Robert J. Drewes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant stock holder challenged decision of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) setting aside a permanent injunction which purported to enjoin respondents water company, its president, and its secretary from transferring, cancelling, or reversing a certain number of shares of the water company's stock. The only respondent on appeal was the California Public Utilities Commission, which intervened below.


OVERVIEW: The stockholder's predecessor bought a certain number of the water company's shares. The commission later declared that the purchase of the water company stock by the stockholder's predecessor was in violation of law and was void. The stockholder obtained an injunction preventing the water company form transferring or cancelling those shares except to him. The commission intervened in the action and it was on the commission's motion that the subject injunction was set aside. The court found when the stockholder obtained his injunction, the commission's order holding the acquisition of the subject shares to be void was in effect. The injunction, then, was calculated to interfere with the commission in the performance of its official duties in violation of Cal. Pub. Util. Code § 1759. Moreover, that same statute gave the state supreme court exclusive jurisdiction to review orders of the commission. Therefore, the injunction was properly set aside. The court rejected the stockholder's due process argument because he had the opportunity to appear before the commission or to ask for recission of its decision.


OUTCOME: The order setting aside the injunction was affirmed.

 

1108 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Ganz v. Justice Court for Arvin-Lamont Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 1068


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


273 Cal. App. 2d 612; 78 Cal. Rptr. 348; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2207



June 2, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Kern County. Walter Osborn, Jr., Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to forbid the Justice Court for the Arvin-Lamont Judicial District from using its jury panel for a specific criminal trial.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Kern County (California) which dismissed defendant's writ of prohibition to forbid the Justice Court of the Arvin-Lamont Judicial District from using its general jury panel for defendant's trial for failure to yield the right of way at an intersection in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 21800(b). Defendant contended that the jury panel was not a fair cross-section of the population.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed a writ of prohibition to forbid the lower court from using its general jury panel in defendant's criminal trial for failure to yield the right of way at an intersection. Defendant challenged the entire jury panel pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1058 on the ground that there were material departures from the forms prescribed in respect to the drawing and return of the jury, Cal. Penal Code § 1059. Defendant claimed that a writ of prohibition was a proper remedy and that the percentage of persons with Spanish surnames was substantially below that of the general population from which the jury panel was drawn. The court found that an action for prohibition based on discrimination in jury selection was a proper remedy. The court, however, affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the writ, holding that any errors and irregularities in making up the jury list did not invalidate the list where defendant was Jewish and not a member of the group allegedly discriminated against. The court concluded that there was no evidence of intentional discrimination and that that defendant had not shown that he would be deprived of a fair trial by an impartial jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal.

People v. Collins


Crim. No. 5237


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


273 Cal. App. 2d 486; 78 Cal. Rptr. 151; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2191



May 28, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 23, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County. Richard A. Schoenig, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of marijuana.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which convicted him of possession of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of possession of marijuana. On appeal, he asserted that his conviction was unconstitutional because he used marijuana to practice his religious beliefs. The court rejected this argument because defendant did not worship or sanctify marijuana but used it to enhance his ability to engage in meditative communication and to attain spiritual peace through union with God. The court found that because the use of marijuana was not indispensable to the pursuit of his faith it was within the state's authority to prohibit its use by defendant and to enforce the state's interest in preventing the use of marijuana.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

1110 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Kirk v. Board of Regents


Civ. No. 25734


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


273 Cal. App. 2d 430; 78 Cal. Rptr. 260; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2184



May 28, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On July 7, 1969, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 13, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Monroe Friedman and Lewis E. Lercara, Judges.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Board of Regents of the University of California to reclassify a student from a "nonresident" to a "resident" status.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to complaint sustained affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant student initiated a proceeding in mandamus to compel respondent, the Board of Regents of the University of California, to reclassify her from a "nonresident" to a "resident" student. She appealed the judgment of the Superior court of Alameda County (California), which sustained a demurrer of the board and dismissed the student's case.


OVERVIEW: The student contended that she qualified as a "resident" student because she became a resident of California through operation of law by her marriage to a California resident on July 1, 1967. She argued that she should be able to retroactively "tack on" her husband's period of residency in order to meet a one-year residence requirement as of the first day of classes in the fall of 1967, and maintained that certain constitutional rights she possessed were being violated by the board's treatment of her as a "nonresident" student. The court held that the regulation classifying students as residents or nonresidents for tuition purposes was not arbitrary or unreasonable and had a rational nexus with the State of California's objective of providing publicly financed institutions of higher learning. Further, the court ruled that the student was not being denied the equal protection of the laws, and the board was not running afoul of the privileges and immunities clause, because residents and nonresidents could be treated differently where there were valid reasons for doing so. The court therefore affirmed the trial court's judgment of dismissal of the student's complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the student's action.

 

1111 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Saunders v. Los Angeles


Civ. No. 32731


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


273 Cal. App. 2d 407; 78 Cal. Rptr. 236; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2180



May 27, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 23, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Robert S. Thompson, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the City of Los Angeles and its board of police commissioners to issue a renewal license to operate an automobile repair business.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee sought review of the decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his application for a peremptory writ of mandamus to compel respondents, a city and a Board of Police Commissioners, to renew his license to operate an automobile repair business.


OVERVIEW: The licensee operated an automobile repair business an applied for a renewal of his license. A hearing examiner denied his application on the basis of prior criminal convictions and the Board adopted the findings. The licensee filed a petition for writ of mandamus to compel the city and board to issue the license and the trial court denied the writ. The licensee challenged the decision and the court affirmed. The procedures used by the board were not improper because the standards for denial of a new permit, renewal of a permit, or revocation of an existing permit were the same. Regardless of whether the board proceeded under Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code art. 1, §§ 103.31(c), 103.34 or Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code art. 3, §§ 103.31(c), 103.35 the same review applied, and the same result would have followed. The standards were not indefinite or vague and broader protections that applied in First Amendment causes did not apply. The hearing officer properly took into account that the crimes committed by the licensee had no relation to his repair business. Finally, neither the ordinances nor their application interfered with his right to liberty or his pursuit of happiness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the licensee's petition for a writ of mandamus against the city and board.

 

1112 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Martinez v. Harris


Civ. No. 25103


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


273 Cal. App. 2d 385; 78 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2178



May 27, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied June 26, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEALS from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County in an automobile accident case and from an order granting defendant's motion for a new trial. Donald K. Quayle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from judgment dismissed; order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants injured parties and the heirs of deceased parties sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted cross-appellant driver's motion for a new trial in an action arising from an accident involving three automobiles. The driver appealed the judgment against him.


OVERVIEW: The driver was involved in a three car accident when he was struck by a drunk driver, propelling him into appellants' car, killing the other driver and front seat passenger and injuring the two back seat passengers. Appellants filed suit against the drunk driver and the driver. The jury awarded appellants damages against both the drunk driver and the driver. The driver filed motions for new trials, which the superior court granted. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the order granting the new trial complied with the provisions of Cal Civ. Proc. Code § 657 and that the record supported the order. The driver's cross-appeal was dismissed. The court held that the specific reason given by the trial judge for the new trial, that he did not believe the evidence presented by appellants in support of their case against the driver, was sufficient to justify the new trial, when he observed the witnesses testify and considered their biases and ability to see the events of the accident in resolving the inconsistencies in the evidence. The record lacked proof of the driver's negligence, appellants' evidence was contradicted and disbelieved, and it showed the driver exercised due care.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's order granting the new trial. The court dismissed the driver's cross-appeal from the judgment and ordered the driver to recover costs on appeal.

 

1113 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Olson v. Clifton


Civ. No. 25423


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


273 Cal. App. 2d 359; 78 Cal. Rptr. 296; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2175



May 26, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from judgments of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Gerald S. Chargin, Judge.

Action for damages for personal injuries suffered when the automobile in which plaintiff was riding crashed into a power pole.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of nonsuit affirmed; appeal from purported judgment on motion for directed verdict dismissed. Judgment of nonsuit affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff passenger appealed decisions of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted the motions by defendants, the vehicle's driver and owner, for a directed verdict and a judgment of nonsuit in an action to recover damages for personal injuries suffered in an automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: The passenger was injured when the car in which she was riding struck a power pole. She filed a personal injury action against the car's driver and the car's owner. The trial court granted defendants' motions for a nonsuit following testimony on liability. The trial court also granted a motion for directed judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the nonsuit and dismissed the appeal from the purported directed verdict. The evidence did not sustain a finding that the passenger's injuries proximately resulted from the wilful misconduct of defendants under the guest law, Cal. Veh. Code § 17158. The trial court correctly ruled as a matter of law that the accident occurred while the passenger was riding as a guest "upon a highway," and the guest law applied. The court held that there was no evidence to show a quarrel or anger on the part of the driver. If there were some reason to support the necessary state of mind for a deliberate foolhardy act on the part of the driver, it would have been fully explored during the case in chief. The facts of the case, including losing control of a car and hitting an object off the side of the roadway, were insufficient to establish wilful misconduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of nonsuit in the passenger's personal injury action against the car's driver and owner. The court dismissed the appeal from the purported judgment on the motion for directed verdict.

 

1114 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Yeoman v. Department of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 9160


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


273 Cal. App. 2d 71; 78 Cal. Rptr. 251; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2142



May 16, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Paul G. Mast, Judge pro tem. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Department of Motor Vehicles to issue a school bus driver certificate or to show cause why it should not do so.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment granting writ reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state agency sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), in favor of respondent school bus driver and which ordered, by writ of mandate, that the state agency issue a school bus driver certificate to the school bus driver.


OVERVIEW: After the school bus driver was convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor, the state agency revoked his school bus driver certificate. The school bus driver filed a petition for a writ of mandate to direct the state agency to issue him a school bus driver certificate or to show cause why it should not do so. The trial court held that the school bus driver was unconstitutionally deprived of his school bus driver certificate. On appeal the court found that school bus drivers were properly given a special classification, and the California Board of Education could properly require in school bus operators qualifications of good character and higher standards of driver performance than those imposed for the issuance of general operators' licenses. This was in order to protect the safety of children who were passengers on school buses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court which had found in favor of the school bus driver.

 

1115 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Montgomery Ward & Co. v. State Board of Equalization


Civ. No. 25220


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


272 Cal. App. 2d 728; 78 Cal. Rptr. 373; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2332



May 12, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 29, 1969, and Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied August 6, 1969. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco. Alvin E. Weinberger, Judge.

Action by a retailer for recovery of use taxes paid.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment for plaintiff affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retailer sued defendant State Board of Equalization (board) contending that an exaction levied violated the U.S. Constitution and was unauthorized by the California Constitution and the California Sales and Use Tax Law, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code, div. 2, pt. 1, §§ 6001-7176. The Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) entered judgment for the retailer and the board appealed.


OVERVIEW: The board contended that the retailer should have collected use taxes for California on sales of goods delivered on credit at its Oregon and Nevada stores to customers who held charge accounts bearing an address in California. The court held that existing precedents under the due process clause precluded the board from burdening the retailer's Nevada or Oregon business with the collection of use taxes on over-the-counter sales to California residents. The court held that the fact that the retailer elected to carry on an intrastate business in California was purely fortuitous and did not give California the right to reach out and tax or otherwise burden transactions which were entirely dissociated from the local business. In addition, the court held that the discriminatory imposition of a liability to collect the California use tax unwarrantedly burdened the right of the out-of-state retailer to do business with residents of California, in violation of the commerce clause. The court held further that because the effect of the tax was to impose upon the retailer a greater burden than was imposed upon those engaged in similar business, the retailer had been denied equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

1116 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Mancino v. Santa Clara County Flood Control & Water Dist.


Civ. No. 24459


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


272 Cal. App. 2d 678; 77 Cal. Rptr. 679; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2324



May 9, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County. Raymond G. Callaghan, Judge.

Action for trespass and inverse condemnation.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment awarding compensation for damages to property only affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner appealed from the order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted judgment in favor of the property owner in her action filed against defendant, the Santa Clara County Flood Control and Water District (district), for trespass and inverse condemnation. The trial court had instructed the jury that the property owner was not entitled to compensation for a taking of property under a street.


OVERVIEW: The city authorized the district to construct a storm drainage system under the surface of a city street and to erect an entryway, which was a large concrete box that extended above ground level and provided access to the control box of the drainage system. The entryway was erected on the street in front of the property owner's home between the curb and the cement sidewalk. The property owner filed an action for trespass and conversion, and after the jury returned a verdict in favor of the property owner, the property owner appealed alleging that the trial court erred in finding and instructing the jury that the property owner was not entitled to compensation for the taking and use of any interest she had in the street. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the street was a public street that was under the dominion and control of the city and that any interest the property owner held to the centerline of the street was subject to the city's right to use the street for the benefit of the public. The court held that the entryway did not interfere with the property owner's reasonable access to her property or her right to light and air.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court awarding the property owner compensation for damages to her property, and directing the jury not to award damages for the taking of property under the street.

 

1117 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California Federation of Teachers v. Oxnard Elementary Schools


Civ. No. 32760


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


272 Cal. App. 2d 514; 77 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2306



May 1, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 25, 1969. Peters, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County. William A. Reppy, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to require an elementary school district to deal directly with a teacher's union, to reemploy a teacher, and to declare unconstitutional the Winton Act which sets up negotiating councils.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, federation of teachers (teachers) and its executive secretary, challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied their petition for writ of mandamus to require appellee school district to deal directly with a teacher's union, to reemploy the executive secretary as a teacher, and to declare unconstitutional the Winton Act, Cal. Educ. Code §§ 13080-13088 (1965).


OVERVIEW: The teachers and the executive secretary filed a petition for writ of mandamus requesting the school district be ordered to stop discriminating against the teachers, permit the teachers to deal directly with the school district, and require the reemployment of the executive secretary, who had been on a prolonged leave of absence. The teachers also sought a declaration that the Winton Act (Act), Cal. Educ. Code §§13080- 13088, was unconstitutional because it classified certified teachers separately from uncertified teachers and required they present employment issues collectively. The trial court concluded that there was insufficient evidence of discrimination to warrant a writ or to require reemployment of the executive secretary, and that the Act was constitutional, requiring the teachers to address employment issues through a negotiating council. The court agreed, holding that there was no constitutional violation by segregating certified teachers through legislation mandating the bargaining procedures with their employer in that they were treated equally within the class. Substantial evidence supported the conclusions as to discrimination and reemployment of the secretary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order denying the writ of mandamus and declaring the Winton Act constitutional.

 

1118 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Windsor Mills v. Richard B. Smith, Inc.


Civ. No. 9072


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


272 Cal. App. 2d 336; 77 Cal. Rptr. 300; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2280



April 28, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On June 3, 1969, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of dismissal of the Superior Court of Orange County. Herbert S. Herlands, Judge.

Action by a materialman against a landowner to foreclose a mechanic's lien.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer to third amended complaint was sustained without leave to amend affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff materialman appealed the order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) that sustained defendant landowner's demurrer to the materialman's third amended complaint without leave to amend. The materialman brought the action against the landowner to foreclose on a mechanic's lien.


OVERVIEW: The materialman supplied carpeting for a house on behalf of a contractor for the landowner's tract homes. In an affidavit, the materialman's attorney averred that he gave the landowner's attorney telephonic notice of the mechanic's lien. The trial court dismissed that action based on the materialman's failure to append the written notice to the third amended complaint, required by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1193. The materialman argued that the telephone call from its attorney to the landowner's attorney constituted substantial compliance with the pre-lien written notice, that the landowner should be estopped from relying on § 1193, and that § 1193 discriminated against materialmen who installed carpeting and was thus unconstitutional. The court rejected those arguments and affirmed the trial court's order, finding that § 1193 required a written pre-lien notice in order to advise an owner that his property could be subjected to a lien arising from a contract to which he was not a party. Consequently, the materialman could not show that an estoppel was warranted. Finally, there was no constitutional compulsion for special treatment of materialmen furnishing carpeting.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court sustaining the landowner's demurrer to the materialman's third amended complaint without leave to amend.

 

1119 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Tharp


Crim. No. 14906


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


272 Cal. App. 2d 268; 78 Cal. Rptr. 412; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2268



April 24, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 18, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Vernon P. Spencer, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of marijuana and possession of a restricted dangerous drug.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of possession of marijuana and possession of a restricted dangerous drug.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions, alleging that certain evidence was inadmissible as the result of an illegal arrest or illegal search and that the evidence was insufficient to prove knowing possession of drugs. Defendant also contended that his waiver of a jury trial was ineffective and that the statute prohibiting and punishing the possession of marijuana was unconstitutional. The court rejected defendant's arguments. The police officers were justified in relying on information from apartment managers to arrest defendant because the managers were objective private citizens who were acting openly for the purpose of aiding law enforcement. The information gave the officers reason to believe that defendant had been in knowing possession of the marijuana. Accordingly, the arresting officer was justified in arresting defendant. The resulting search of defendant's car was incident to the lawful arrest and was sufficiently restricted in scope to be incidental to the arrest. Defendant's waiver of a jury trial was effective because there was no evidence that he or his counsel was misled. Finally, Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

1120 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Neese


Crim. No. 15652


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


272 Cal. App. 2d 235; 77 Cal. Rptr. 314; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2263



April 23, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 22, 1969. Thompson, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted. Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 18, 1969. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Howard D. McClain, Judge.

Prosecution for the possession of a concealable weapon by a former felon.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of having in his possession a revolver capable of being concealed on his person in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 12021.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of having possession of a revolver capable of being concealed on his person and he had previously been convicted of a felony. After prosecution witnesses against defendant admitted that they had falsely told the police that defendant had the weapon in his possession, the prosecution introduced evidence of the witnesses' prior statements to the police. Defendant failed to object that this evidence could not be used as substantive evidence, and did not request that a limiting instruction be given. Defendant was convicted of the possession offense and the court affirmed. Defendant's failure to object did not preclude him from raising the substantive use of prior inconsistent statements as an issue on appeal because at the time of his trial, a statute permitting such use of the evidence was not held to be unconstitutional until after the trial was concluded. However, defendant was not prejudiced by admission of the prior inconsistent statements without a limiting instruction and his conviction was affirmed because the inconsistent statements were not the sole basis for the verdict, and there was other substantial evidence supporting the guilty verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of possessing a revolver capable of being concealed on his person was affirmed.

 

1121 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


California School Employees Assn. v. Sequoia Union High School Dist.


Civ. No. 25397


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


272 Cal. App. 2d 98; 77 Cal. Rptr. 187; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2249



April 21, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 18, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Mateo County denying a preliminary injunction. Joseph S. Huberty, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairman of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of San Mateo County, California, denied a preliminary injunction to plaintiff school employees association against defendant school district. The injunction was sought to restrain the school district from contracting for vending machines to dispense foodstuffs prepared off the school premises to pupils within the district. The school employees association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The school district (district) discharged the food service employees and negotiated with a vending company to supply fool through vending machines. The court held that the school employees association had standing because its interest was in establishing for the benefit of its displaced members that the food services to be rendered under the proposed contract were a mere substitute for those which were discontinued and as such were illegal because they were to be performed by nonclassified employees. The court held that Cal. Educ. Code §§ 17052, 17101 demonstrated that the Legislature intended that the sale of food through vending machines was to be governed by the chapter of the code under review. Since the law expressly contemplated that vending machines could be used for dispensing some of the food which the district was authorized to sell and serve, there was no inherent reason why all such food and drink could not be so dispensed. The court also held that the fact that a private entrepreneur made a profit from the equipment that it furnished did not make the school's contribution to the food service process a gift to him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

1123 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wright


Crim. No. 15394


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


272 Cal. App. 2d 53; 76 Cal. Rptr. 859; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2243



April 17, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 11, 1969. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County. W. Preston Butcher, Judge.

Prosecution for trespass and for battery on a peace officer.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) that convicted him by jury trial of trespass in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 602(j) (a misdemeanor), a lesser included offense of the crime of burglary. Defendant was also convicted of battery on a peace officer in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 243.


OVERVIEW: Based on a reported burglary and a description of a suspect, the police officer stopped defendant and asked him questions. Defendant struck the police officer when asked to cooperate with a pat down search for weapons. Although the state charged defendant with a felony of burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459, he was convicted of a misdemeanor of trespass in violation of § 602(j). Consecutive sentences were imposed by the trial court against defendant of six months in the county jail for the trespass and for the required term in the state prison for the battery. The court affirmed the contested judgment because sufficient evidence supported both convictions. The court found that the police possessed reasonable grounds for the arrest and that defendant committed a battery under § 243. Defendant could not challenge the trial court decision to instruct the jury on trespass because he invited that error by requesting that instruction. The court rejected defendant's contention that § 243 was unconstitutional because defendant reasonably should have known that the police officer was performing his duties when defendant was stopped, questioned, and the search occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment challenged by defendant that convicted him by jury trial of trespass and battery on a peace officer.

 

1124 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re Wilkerson


Crim. No. 4984


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


271 Cal. App. 2d 798; 77 Cal. Rptr. 340; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2441



April 14, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied May 8, 1969, and Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied June 11, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: PROCEEDING in habeas corpus to command the Adult Authority to grant a parole.



DISPOSITION: Order to show cause discharged; writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed a request for a writ of habeas corpus to command respondent California Adult Authority to grant a parole.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was "wanted" in New Jersey to serve the unexpired portion of a sentence after a revoked parole. He sought transfer to the authorities in New Jersey. New Jersey declined to accept the prisoner unless and until he had either been paroled or discharged in California. The prisoner requested that the court command the Adult Authority to grant him a parole so that New Jersey would accept him. The prisoner argued that he was entitled to parole, which, he argued, would have been granted but for the existence of two allegedly invalid prior convictions. The court found that no facts showed an abuse of discretion by the Adult Authority. There was no proper basis to make a factual determination of the prisoner's contention that two of his several prior convictions were invalid. It was the exclusive function of the Adult Authority to make discretionary judgments as to whether and when an eligible prisoner would be granted parole or discharged within the limits fixed by law. As long as it could be said that the Adult Authority had not abused that broad discretion, the courts would not interfere. The Adult Authority could not compel the State of New Jersey to accept the prisoner.


OUTCOME: The order to show cause was discharged. The petition for a writ of habeas corpus was denied.

 

1125 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


American Smelting & Refining Co. v. County of Contra Costa


Civ. No. 24762


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


271 Cal. App. 2d 437; 77 Cal. Rptr. 570; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2401



April 4, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for Rehearing was Denied on April 29, 1969, and the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied July 2, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County. Joseph Genser, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus by a smelting company seeking cancellation of a property tax assessment on imported metal-bearing ores and for injunctive relief restraining the collection of taxes levied on the assessment.



DISPOSITION: Reversed with directions. Judgment granting writ and injunction reversed with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California) granted plaintiff refining company a peremptory writ of mandate ordering defendants to cancel a property tax assessment, and an injunction permanently restraining the collection of property taxes levied on that assessment, and defendants, county and its officials, appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, county and its officials, appealed the judgment that granted plaintiff refining company a peremptory writ of mandate ordering defendants to cancel an allegedly illegal property tax assessment, and an injunction permanently restraining the collection of property taxes levied on that assessment. The assessment involved imported metal-bearing ores and concentrates, and refined metals, including gold, subject to United States Customs Bond, similar materials of foreign origin, including gold in petitioner's bonded warehouse, but not subject to bond, and gold of domestic origin. The court reversed. It found that the ores and minerals in question, with the exception of those appropriated to fulfill plaintiff's obligation to reexport, were not protected by the Import-Export Clause, or the Commerce Clause; and that neither the federal laws and regulations enacted for the regulation of foreign commerce and the protection of the federal revenues, nor the laws and regulations restricting traffic in gold gave the ores and minerals an immunity from nondiscriminatory local taxation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

 

1126 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Alta-Dena Dairy v. County of San Diego


Civ. No. 9150


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


271 Cal. App. 2d 66; 76 Cal. Rptr. 510; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 2358



March 25, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 10, 1969, and Respondent's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 21, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County. Gerald C. Thomas, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus, certiorari and prohibition against a county and its Director of Public Health in response to an order directing a dairy to discontinue the production of raw milk for sale until proving it free of pathogenic organisms.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part. Judgment of dismissal after sustaining demurrer to third amended petition affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff dairy sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained a demurrer brought by defendants, county and its director of public health, and dismissed the dairy's petition for writs of mandamus, certiorari, and prohibition brought in response to the director's order to discontinue its production of raw milk until it was free of pathogenic organisms.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to a public health code, the county director of public health ordered the dairy to discontinue its production of raw milk for sale and to pasteurize all milk that it produced for distribution until it no longer contained bacteria. The dairy conceded that its raw milk contained a small quantity of bacteria but that it was not harmful for human consumption. The dairy brought an action for mandamus based upon the denial of substantive and procedural due process because the action taken by the director was without notice or hearing and unlawfully precluded the dairy from the enjoyment of a valuable property right. The trial court dismissed the dairy's petition. On review, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part the decision. The court affirmed dismissal of certain causes of action which were redundant. However, the court held that the language of the health code in which the director relied upon set up no machinery or procedure for reviewing the order and made no provision for a hearing. The court held that it was not an adequate procedural remedy of review. The dairy was entitled to a trial de novo consistent with the fundamental principles of due process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the dairy's petition for mandamus and remanded the cause for trial. The court also affirmed the dismissal of certain causes of action brought by the dairy which contained the same factual allegations but sought relief on different theories of law.

Hamilton v. Municipal Court for Berkeley-Albany Judicial Dist.


Civ. No. 25126


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


270 Cal. App. 2d 797; 76 Cal. Rptr. 168; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1592; 33 A.L.R.3d 1029



March 20, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied May 14, 1969. Peters, J., was of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Leonard J. Dieden, Judge.

Proceeding in prohibition to prevent a prosecution for criminal contempt for wilful disobedience of a pretrial municipal court order restricting the release of information pending a trial.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant contemnors challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that found the contemnors violated a pretrial order prohibiting the discussion of the merits of the issues and evidence to have been raised in an upcoming trial that stemmed from a student protest of armed forces recruiters on campus. The contemnors were found guilty of criminal contempt, Cal. Penal Code § 166.


OVERVIEW: The pretrial order prohibited the parties from directly or indirectly releasing or discussing the merits of any of the facts and issues involved in an upcoming trial. However, the contemnors were allowed to identify the parties, the contents of the complaint against them, and their plea. The court affirmed the judgment that found the contemnors violated the order. The court held that the order did not violate the contemnors right of free speech because the trial court could properly ensure a fair trial by taking certain steps to control pretrial publicity of evidence and facts likely to have been presented to the jury. The court held that the order need not have been drawn to limit disclosure of specific information that could have had a prejudicial effect on the trial because that would have been impossible to determine. All that was required was enough specificity so that the parties knew what was proscribed. The court also found that the order was issued based upon the clear and present danger test where there was wide scale student disorder at the university. Finally, the order was not overly broad when it was also directed at potential witnesses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that held the contemnors were guilty of criminal contempt for violating a valid pretrial order that prohibited discussion of the merits and evidence of the issues that were to have been raised in a trial.

 

1128 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bloom


Crim. No. 15296


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


270 Cal. App. 2d 731; 76 Cal. Rptr. 137; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1582



March 18, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County granting probation after conviction of possessing marijuana. Timothy I. O'Reilly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order from the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which granted defendant probation after his conviction for possession of marijuana.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped after a report that suspicious juveniles were hitchhiking on a road. Defendant and a co-defendant were of questionable age, were slightly unkempt, and defendant wore long hair. They gave a police officer identification that showed both were more than 18 years old. In the course of a conversation, defendant opened his bag to show the officer a school book. The officer observed marijuana through a tear in the bag, and defendant and his co-defendant were arrested. Defendant argued that the officer had no right to detain them while waiting for a radio check on the authenticity of the identifications they had furnished. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment of conviction because there was nothing unreasonable about the officer's calling for a check on their identification or in detaining them for a few moments while awaiting a report. The court also held that there also was nothing improper in his questioning of the suspects or in his making the joking statements that led defendant to open his bag. The court held that circumstances short of probable cause to make an arrest justified the officer's stopping of defendant on the streets for questioning.


OUTCOME: The order granting defendant probation was affirmed.

 

1129 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gentry


Crim. No. 4702


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


270 Cal. App. 2d 462; 76 Cal. Rptr. 336; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1548



March 6, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied April 1, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Albert H. Mundt, Judge.

Prosecution for inflicting cruel or inhuman corporal punishment upon a small child.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) entered judgment upon a jury verdict convicting defendant of inflicting cruel or inhuman corporal punishment upon a child in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 273d. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The victim was a one and one-half-year-old child. Defendant was the paramour of the victim's mother and was living in the mother's home. The evidence tended to show that, after a night of drinking, defendant severely beat the victim. Several witnesses also testified that defendant had previously mistreated the victim. At the sheriff's office, defendant agreed when one of the officers suggested that defendant had beaten the victim, when the victim awakened and defendant couldn't quiet the victim down. After the victim's older sibling was unable at trial to remember the events on the night in question, a police officer was allowed to testify based on a written report containing a statement previously made by the sibling. On appeal, defendant argued that the trial court erred in admitting the officer's testimony to establish the sibling's past recollection recorded, in admitting prior consistent statements to rehabilitate a witness after a charge of recent fabrication, and in failing to give a necessarily included offense instruction. The appellate court affirmed defendant's conviction. There had been no miscarriage of justice, and the evidence supported defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of inflicting cruel or inhuman corporal punishment upon a child.

 

1130 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Eisen v. Regents of University of Cal.


Civ. No. 24987


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


269 Cal. App. 2d 696; 75 Cal. Rptr. 45; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1690; 37 A.L.R.3d 1300



February 17, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County. William J. McGuiness, Judge.

Action for injunctive relief to restrain the University from opening to the public the registration statements of student organizations.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained without leave to amend affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff student sought review of a judgment of dismissal by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), entered on an order sustaining, without leave to amend, the demurrer of defendant university. The student had sought injunctive relief to restrain the university from opening to the public the registration statements of student organizations.


OVERVIEW: The student was the officer of a student organization. As a condition of registration the university required the organization to submit a statement of its purpose and the names of its officers, and the student's name was submitted. Following a request by a member of the public for this information, the university adopted a policy that registration statements were records open to inspection. The court affirmed the dismissal of the student's complaint. The court questioned the student's assertion of a right to keep anonymous his relationship as an officer of the organization in the absence of any immediate and direct threats of physical or other danger. The rights of rank and file members to remain anonymous were clearly delineated and if the information concerning the identity of the organization and the names of its officers were disclosed, the additional disclosure of the names of the rank and file members would not have served a legitimate state purpose. The court held that the compelling interest of the public in being able to ascertain the information contained in the registration statement outweighed any minimal infringement of the student's First Amendment rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of dismissal after demurrer was sustained without leave to amend, in the student's complaint against the university seeking to prohibit the university from disclosing to the public the registration statements of student organizations.

 

1131 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Barkin v. Board of Optometry


Civ. No. 31648


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


269 Cal. App. 2d 714; 75 Cal. Rptr. 337; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1692



February 17, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 17, 1969. Peters, J., Tobriner, J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Richard L. Wells, Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus to compel the Board of Optometry to set aside its decision imposing discipline for violation of certain Business and Professions Code sections.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment denying writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner optometrist started an optical service for union members and their families, and respondent Board of Optometry found that the optometrist's advertisements violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §§ 651, 651.3, 3125, and 3129. The Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denied the optometrist's petition for a writ of mandate to compel the board to set aside its decision, and he appealed.


OVERVIEW: The optometrist placed a large sign on the building housing his business and advertised his services of "prepaid" (free) eye exams and discounted glasses through brochures distributed to union members. The trial court found that the board's findings were supported by the weight of the evidence. The trial court did not allow the optometrist to introduce evidence to show that the board was biased, and the appellate court agreed because he had not offered evidence of bias during the agency hearing. On appeal, the court found that he advertised to the public even though only union members received the information. The court found that his business was not a "medical service plan" exempt from the advertising prohibition of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 651.3 because there was no prepayment for the service, and that he violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 3129 by advertising prices for optical services. Practicing under a company name violated Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 3125 even though he disclosed his name to clients. The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the board.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which had refused to compel the Board of Optometry to set aside its decision disciplining the optometrist and had discharged an alternative writ.

 

1132 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


In re K.


Civ. No. 9183


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


269 Cal. App. 2d 646; 75 Cal. Rptr. 136; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1685



February 13, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Orange County placing a minor on probation and releasing him to the custody of his parents. Bruce W. Sumner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a minor, appealed an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which found that defendant struck a person with a motor vehicle, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23104, and placed defendant on probation, in juvenile delinquency proceedings instituted by the chief probation officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was charged with striking a person with a motor vehicle, in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23104. In delinquency proceedings, the trial court used a standard of proof of preponderance of evidence, rather than proof beyond a reasonable doubt, to support its finding that defendant had committed the acts with which he was charged. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that even though defendant's alleged delinquent act would have been chargeable as a criminal offense had he been an adult, juvenile delinquency proceedings were nonetheless considered to be inherently civil, and not criminal in nature; thus, the preponderance of evidence standard used in other civil proceedings was properly applied to defendant's case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's adjudication of defendant as delinquent, and the resulting order placing him on probation.

 

1133 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


Myers v. Arcata Union High School Dist.


Civ. No. 24453


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


269 Cal. App. 2d 549; 75 Cal. Rptr. 68; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1674



February 10, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 9, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from an order of the Superior Court of Humboldt County. William G. Watson, Jr., Judge.

Proceeding in mandamus by a minor high school student against school authorities to compel his reinstatement.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Order granting writ affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, school district and its governing board, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Humboldt County (California) which directed an order to issue a writ of mandamus in favor of plaintiff student in a case involving suspension of the student.


OVERVIEW: The student was suspended from attending school because of the length of his hair. He brought an action in mandamus, through his mother to compel reinstatement. The school district and its governing board appealed from the trial court's order directing issuance of a writ of mandate as prayed. The school district and the school board contended that the dress policy at the high school, to the effect that extremes of hair styles were not acceptable, was a reasonable exercise of the governing board's rulemaking power in the area of student discipline and that it was not constitutionally unenforceable by reason of its language. The court stated that the school district policy concerning extremes of hair styles restrained the student's freedom of expression guaranteed by the U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. In this area, the standards of permissible statutory vagueness were strict and the government could regulate only with narrow specificity. Therefore, the court affirmed the order directing issuance of a writ of mandate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that directed an issuance of a writ of mandate in favor of the student. The court held that the school district's and the school board's dress policy concerning extremes of hair styles was over broad and vague and hindered the student's freedom of expression.

 

1134 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Weitzer


Crim. No. 6776


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


269 Cal. App. 2d 274; 75 Cal. Rptr. 318; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1644



January 30, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's Petition for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied April 30, 1969. Peters, J., and Tobriner, J., were of the Opinion that the Petition Should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment (order granting probation) of the Superior Court of Alameda County. Robert L. Bostick, Judge.

Prosecution for possession of marijuana.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted him probation following his conviction of possession of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11530 after a bench trial based on the transcript of his preliminary examination.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was stopped for a minor traffic violation. The arresting officer learned that there was a warrant outstanding for defendant's arrest for his failure to appear in court. While conducting a pat down for weapons, the officer felt an object for which defendant's explanation was unbelievable. Defendant appealed when he was convicted for possession of the marijuana contained in the object seized. The arrest warrant bore the facsimile rubber stamped signature of the issuing judge. The court held that: (1) failure to appear arrest warrants were exempt from the signature requirement of Cal. Penal Code § 815 because they did not involve the exercise of judicial discretion and could be delegated to ministerial officers of the court; (2) no abuse of discretion was demonstrated by the application of the local court's general order authorizing nighttime execution to the warrant which was issued and executed in the judge's name; (3) the use of the facsimile signature where it was affixed under the supervision and by the direction of the signator was not improper; and (4) any subsequent determination that the warrant was invalid did not render the arrest illegal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for possession of marijuana.

 

1135 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mason


Crim. No. 14033


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


269 Cal. App. 2d 311; 74 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1647



January 30, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment (order granting probation) of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County. Orlando H. Rhodes, Judge.

Prosecution for kidnaping.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which, after a jury trial wherein he was found guilty of kidnapping, Cal. Penal Code § 207, and rape, Cal. Penal Code § 261(4), with prior felony convictions, denied his motion for a new trial and which, sua sponte, dismissed the charge of rape, suspended the kidnapping proceedings, and granted five years' probation with the first six months in jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant got out of his car, grabbed the arm of a waitress who was walking home, had her to get in the car, threatened her life, stopped at a dirt lot, and accomplished an act of sexual intercourse with her. Defendant asked her name and then stated that he thought he got the wrong girl. Slides taken from the witness showed human spermatozoa, and there were seminal stains on defendant's shorts and trousers at the time of his arrest. Defendant's driver alleged that they picked up a girl, drove to a lot, they got out, and he drove away. The court affirmed. There was no constitutional violation of reading an indictment to the jury that referred to defendant's prior conviction or of presenting evidence of the prior convictions to the jury at the same time that the proof of the substantive crime was presented; a bifurcated trial was unnecessary. Further, the court followed the Theodore rule as to proof of the prior convictions; that is, in the absence of other contrary testimony, the identity of names, coupled with proper proof of prior convictions, was sufficient to sustain a finding that defendant was the person involved in the earlier cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

Adoption of Runyon


Civ. No. 12014


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


268 Cal. App. 2d 918; 74 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1759



January 21, 1969 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County. Robert W. Cole, Judge.

Proceeding to adopt a minor child.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment dismissing petition affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foster parents sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (Cal.), which dismissed their petition for adoption of a minor child. A motion filed by respondent welfare department to dismiss their petition was granted by the trial court, which concluded that Cal. Civil Code § 224n barred appellants from adopting the child.


OVERVIEW: The child was relinquished shortly after his birth to the welfare department. He was placed in the home of appellants as a foster child for observation and care. After the department removed the child from appellants' home and placed him with prospective adoptive parents, appellants filed their petition for adoption. The trial court dismissed the petition. Appellants asserted on appeal that the legislature did not intend to deprive the superior court of authority to entertain a petition for adoption of an adoption agency minor by other than agency selectees. The court found that appellants failed to offer any authority for that statement. Appellants contended that § 224n was unconstitutional because in denying them as foster parents the right to adopt an agency child, they were being denied the equal protection of the law and due process. The court found that the provision in § 224n which prohibited anyone but the prospective adoptive parents selected by the adoption agency from adopting a child relinquished to such agency was a reasonable one. The agency should be free to make a determination of the suitability of a home for the child. The statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1137 of 2002 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rowden


Crim. No. 3196


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


268 Cal. App. 2d 868; 74 Cal. Rptr. 448; 1969 Cal. App. LEXIS 1751



January 17, 1969 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied February 7, 1969.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL from a judgment of the Superior Court of Orange County. Robert Gardner, Judge.

Prosecution for robbery.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed. Judgment of conviction of first degree robbery affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which convicted him of robbery in the first degree. Defendant claimed that he was denied a speedy trial and his right against self-incrimination was violated.


OVERVIEW: Defendant failed to appear for trial on a charge of robbery because he was on trial in another county on a similar charge and was convicted. The trial was reset and defendant was convicted of first-degree robbery and sought review. Defendant claimed that Cal. Penal Code § 1381 was unconstitutional because it violated defendant's Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination, and that he was denied the speedy trial guaranteed by Cal. Penal Code § 1382. The court affirmed the judgment and held that: (1) defendant was not compelled to testify against himself when he had to inform the trial court that a charge was pending against him in another county; (2) the privilege against self-incrimination related only to "testimonial compulsion;" (3) the speedy trial provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 1381 that guaranteed a speedy trial did not apply to defendant because he was already serving time for another offense; and (4) defendant failed to raise the speedy trial issue in the trial court and the issue was waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted defendant of first-degree robbery.
